Company</a> (1799\xe2\x80\x931881)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dutch_East_India_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The direct translation of the Dutch name <i>Verenigde Oost-Indische Compagnie</i> is "United East-India Company". For the VOC\'s different English-language <a href="/wiki/Trade_name" title="Trade name">trade names</a>, see articles: <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_India_companies_(disambiguation)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="East India companies (disambiguation) (page does not exist)">East India companies</a>; Greater India; <a href="/wiki/East_India" title="East India">East India</a>; <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a>; Dutch East Indies; <a href="/wiki/Dutch_India" title="Dutch India">Dutch India</a>; <a href="/wiki/Voorcompagnie" title="Voorcompagnie">Voorcompagnie</a>; List of Dutch East India Company trading posts and settlements.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historically, the Dutch East India Company was a multinational proto-conglomerate (including <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-AtsushiOta_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AtsushiOta-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> shipbuilding, spice production and trade,<sup id="cite_ref-LowyInstitute_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LowyInstitute-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane industry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Shih_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shih-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tseng_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tseng-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">wine industry</a><sup id="cite_ref-Estreicher_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Estreicher-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fourie&amp;vonFintel_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fourie&amp;vonFintel-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Williams_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>) rather than a pure trading company or shipping company.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Voorcompagnie" title="Voorcompagnie">voorcompagnie\xc3\xabn</a></i> (or <i>pre</i>\n\ncompanies<i>) include: <a href="/wiki/Compagnie_van_Verre" title="Compagnie van Verre">Compagnie van Verre</a> (Amsterdam, 1594\xe2\x80\x931598), <a href="/wiki/Voorcompagnie" title="Voorcompagnie">Nieuwe Compagnie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voorcompagnie" title="Voorcompagnie">Eerste Verenigde Compagnie op Oost-Indi\xc3\xab</a> (Amsterdam, 1598\xe2\x80\x931601), <a href="/wiki/Voorcompagnie" title="Voorcompagnie">Oude Oost-Indische Compagnie</a> (Amsterdam, 1598\xe2\x80\x931601), <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Verenigde_Amsterdamse_Compagnie" class="extiw" title="nl:Verenigde Amsterdamse Compagnie">Verenigde Amsterdamse Compagnie</a>, <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nieuwe_of_Tweede_Compagnie" class="extiw" title="nl:Nieuwe of Tweede Compagnie">Nieuwe of Tweede Compagnie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brabantsche_Compagnie" title="Brabantsche Compagnie">Brabantsche Compagnie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nieuwe_Brabantsche_Compagnie" class="mw-redirect" title="Nieuwe Brabantsche Compagnie">Nieuwe Brabantsche Compagnie</a>, <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magelhaensche_Compagnie" class="extiw" title="nl:Magelhaensche Compagnie">Magelhaensche Compagnie/Rotterdamse Compagnie</a>, <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middelburgse_Compagnie" class="extiw" title="nl:Middelburgse Compagnie">Middelburgse Compagnie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veerse_Compagnie" title="Veerse Compagnie">Veerse Compagnie</a> (Zeeland, 1597), <a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Verenigde_Zeeuwse_Compagnie" class="extiw" title="nl:Verenigde Zeeuwse Compagnie">Verenigde Zeeuwse Compagnie</a> (Middelburg &amp; Veere, 1600), <a href="/wiki/Compagnie_van_De_Moucheron" title="Compagnie van De Moucheron">Compagnie van De Moucheron</a> (Zeeland, 1600), and <a href="/wiki/Voorcompagnie" title="Voorcompagnie">Delftse Vennootschap</a>. Niels Steensgaard (</i>The Asian Trade Revolution of the Seventeenth Century<i>, 1973) notes, "the </i>voorcompagnie\xc3\xabn<i> were not <a href="/wiki/Incorporation_(business)" title="Incorporation (business)">incorporated</a>, but were run by a number of </i>bewindhebbers<i>, who were joined together like partners in a simple company, i.e. traded on joint account".</i></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the VOC\'s <a href="/wiki/Board_of_directors" title="Board of directors">board of directors</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the VOC\'s <i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Chief_executives" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief executives">chief executives</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, see: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Taiwan" title="Economic history of Taiwan">Economic history of Taiwan</a> <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_South_Africa" title="Economic history of South Africa">and South Africa</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Edward_Stringham" title="Edward Stringham">Edward Stringham</a> (2015) notes: "Companies with transferable shares date back to classical Rome, but these were usually not enduring endeavors and no considerable <a href="/wiki/Secondary_market" title="Secondary market">secondary market</a> existed (Neal, 1997, p. 61)."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A <a href="/wiki/Public_company" title="Public company">public company</a>, also called a <a href="/wiki/Publicly_traded_company" class="mw-redirect" title="Publicly traded company">publicly traded company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Publicly_held_company" class="mw-redirect" title="Publicly held company">publicly held company</a>, or public corporation. A public company can be listed company (<a href="/wiki/Publicly_listed_company" class="mw-redirect" title="Publicly listed company">publicly listed company</a>) or unlisted company (<a href="/wiki/Unlisted_public_company" title="Unlisted public company">unlisted public company</a>).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The concept of the <a href="/wiki/Exchange_(organized_market)" title="Exchange (organized market)">bourse</a> (or the exchange) was historically \'invented\' (in medieval <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a>) before the birth of formal stock exchanges in the 17th century. Before the VOC era, in terms of historical role, a bourse was not exactly a stock exchange in its modern sense. With the establishment of the Dutch East India Company (VOC) and the rise of Dutch <a href="/wiki/Capital_market" title="Capital market">capital markets</a> in the early 1600s, the \'old\' bourse (a place to trade <a href="/wiki/Commodity_market" title="Commodity market">commodities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Municipal_bond" title="Municipal bond">municipal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Government_bond" title="Government bond">government bonds</a>) found a new purpose \xe2\x80\x93 a formal exchange that specialize in creating and sustaining <a href="/wiki/Secondary_market" title="Secondary market">secondary markets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Corporate_bond" title="Corporate bond">bonds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Share_(finance)" title="Share (finance)">shares</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stock" title="Stock">stock</a>) issued by <a href="/wiki/Corporations" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporations">corporations</a> \xe2\x80\x93 or a modern <a href="/wiki/Stock_exchange" title="Stock exchange">stock exchange</a> as we know it today.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As Remco Raben (2013) noted, "The concept of a \'VOC World\', although implicit in many studies on early modern Dutch ventures in Asia, has received little attention and even less discussion. The term was used most prominently in <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Worden" title="Nigel Worden">Nigel Worden</a> (ed.), <i>Contingent Lives: Social Identity and Material Culture in the VOC World</i> (Rondebosch 2007). ... The webbed character of the VOC World deserves further enquiry. This world should not be conceived as a firmly limited space of interaction or as exclusively \'Dutch\'. Both the linkages between various VOC settlements as well as those to the worlds beyond should be taken into account, as the VOC created patterns of transport and safe spaces that facilitated the exchange of knowledge, goods and people. ... All kinds of persons and groups participated in or made use of the VOC World, forming their own webs. One example are knowledge networks, which have become a topic of research only fairly recently. Another strong case are the networks of exiles as described by Kerry Ward in her aptly titled <i>Networks of Empire</i>&#160;..."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">About the VOC\'s exemplary role as a historical company-state (or a \'CorporNation\' as dubbed by <a href="/wiki/Adam_Hanft" title="Adam Hanft">Adam Hanft</a><sup id="cite_ref-AdamHanft_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdamHanft-56">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>), see Arthur Weststeijn (2014)<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> See also: <a href="/wiki/Mega-corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Mega-corporation">mega-corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_sovereignty" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporate sovereignty">corporate sovereignty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_republic" title="Corporate republic">corporate republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporatocracy" title="Corporatocracy">corporatocracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Company_town" title="Company town">company town</a>, and <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For the pioneering roles of the Dutch Republic and the VOC in history of modern capitalism,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peter_J_2002_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_J_2002-61">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> see articles: History of capitalism; Economic history of the Dutch Republic; Financial history of the Dutch Republic.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A transnational corporation differs from a traditional <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporation</a> in that it does not identify itself with one national home. While traditional multinational corporations are national companies with foreign subsidiaries, transnational corporations spread out their operations in many countries sustaining high levels of local responsiveness. An example of a transnational corporation is the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Royal Dutch Shell</a> corporation whose headquarters may be in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a> (Netherlands) but its registered office and main executive body is headquartered in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, United Kingdom. Another example of a transnational corporation is <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestl\xc3\xa9">Nestl\xc3\xa9</a> who employ senior executives from many countries and try to make decisions from a global perspective rather than from one centralized headquarters. While the VOC <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company_in_Indonesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch East India Company in Indonesia">established its main administrative center</a>, as the second headquarters, in <a href="/wiki/Batavia,_Dutch_East_Indies" title="Batavia, Dutch East Indies">Batavia</a> (Dutch East Indies, 1610\xe2\x80\x931800), the company\'s global headquarters was in Amsterdam (Dutch Republic). Also, the company had <a href="/wiki/List_of_Dutch_East_India_Company_trading_posts_and_settlements" title="List of Dutch East India Company trading posts and settlements">important operations</a> elsewhere.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-160">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was also the Dutch financial innovations that helped lay the foundations for the financial system of the modern world,<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> especially in <a href="/wiki/Corporate_finance" title="Corporate finance">corporate finance</a>, and greatly influenced the financial history of <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="English-speaking countries">English-speaking countries</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland">United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" class="mw-redirect" title="United States of America">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Eric Michael Wilson (2008) notes: "(...) the first wholly cognisable modern limited liability public company was the VOC. The organisational structures and corporate practices of the VOC were closely paralleled by the English East India Company and served as the direct model for all of the later mercantile trading companies of the <a href="/wiki/World-systems_theory" title="World-systems theory">second cycle</a>, including those of Italy, France, Portugal, Denmark, and Brandenburg-Prussia."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-170">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In terms of historical importance, overall influence (including institutional innovations), power, size, competitive capability (profitability), and wealth of the VOC.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-176">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">About the relationship between the stock market and capitalism (<a href="/wiki/Modern_capitalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern capitalism">modern capitalism</a> in particular), American economist <a href="/wiki/Murray_Rothbard" title="Murray Rothbard">Murray Rothbard</a> discussed, "Even in the days before <a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">perestroika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> was never a monolith. Within the <a href="/wiki/Communist_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist countries">Communist countries</a>, the spectrum of socialism ranged from the quasi-market, quasi-<a href="/wiki/Syndicalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Syndicalist">syndicalist</a> system of Yugoslavia to the centralized <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a> of neighboring Albania. One time I asked Professor <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises" title="Ludwig von Mises">von Mises</a>, the great expert on the economics of socialism, at what point on this spectrum of statism would he designate a country as "socialist" or not. At that time, I wasn\'t sure that any definite criterion existed to make that sort of clear-cut judgment. And so I was pleasantly surprised at the clarity and decisiveness of Mises\'s answer. "A stock market," he answered promptly. "A stock market is crucial to the existence of capitalism and <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a>. For it means that there is a functioning market in the exchange of private titles to the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a>. There can be no genuine private ownership of capital without a stock market: there can be no true socialism if such a market is allowed to exist.""<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-191"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-191">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mark Smith (2003) notes: "The first joint-stock companies had actually been created in England in the sixteenth century. These early joint-stock firms, however, possessed only temporary charters from the government, in some cases for one voyage only. (One example was the <a href="/wiki/Muscovy_Company" title="Muscovy Company">Muscovy Company</a>, chartered in England in 1533 for trade with Russia; another, chartered the same year, was a company with the intriguing title Guinea Adventurers.) The Dutch East India Company was the first joint-stock company to have a permanent charter."<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wu Wei Neng (2012) noted: "17th century Amsterdam was the world\'s first modern financial centre \xe2\x80\x94 the city hall, Wisselbank, Beurs (stock exchange), Korenbeurs (commodities exchange), major insurance, brokerage and trading companies were located within a few blocks of each other, along with coffee houses which served as informal trading floors and exchanges that facilitated deal-making. <a href="/wiki/Financial_innovation" title="Financial innovation">Financial innovations</a> such as maritime insurance, retirement pensions, annuities, futures and options, transnational securities listings, mutual funds and modern investment banking had their genesis in 17th and 18th century Amsterdam."<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jan Peter Balkenende: "Ik begrijp niet waarom u er zo negatief en vervelend over doet. Laten we blij zijn met elkaar. Laten we zeggen: \'Nederland kan het weer!\', die VOC-mentaliteit. Over grenzen heen kijken! Dynamiek! Toch?" [Original in Dutch, loosely translated from footage]</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-228"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-228">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As Gelderblom, de Jong, and Jonker (2010) noted: "...The gradual articulation of governing large partnerships was taken a step further by the First United East India Company (<i>Eerste Verenigde Compagnie op Oost-Indi\xc3\xab</i>), formed by a <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">merger</a> between Amsterdam\'s <i>Oude Compagnie</i> and a venture run by Flemish immigrants, the <i>Nieuwe Compagnie</i>, in 1601. (...) In 1597 <a href="/wiki/Johan_Van_Oldenbarnevelt" class="mw-redirect" title="Johan Van Oldenbarnevelt">Van Oldenbarnevelt</a> started pushing for a <a href="/wiki/Consolidation_(business)" title="Consolidation (business)">consolidation</a> because the continuing competition threatened to compromise the <a href="/wiki/Eighty_Years%27_War" title="Eighty Years&#39; War">Dutch fight against Spain and Portugal</a> in Asia (Den Heijer 2005, 41). The companies of Middelburg and Veere followed the Amsterdam example and merged into one <i>Verenigde Zeeuwse Compagnie</i> in 1600. The idea for a merger between the all companies, first considered in 1599, then reappeared, given new momentum by the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/English_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="English East India Company">East India Company in Britain</a>. (...) Negotiations between the Dutch companies took a long time because of conflicting demands. Firstly, the <a href="/wiki/States-General_of_the_Dutch_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="States-General of the Dutch Republic">Estates General</a> wanted the merger to secure a strong Dutch presence in Asia. The hot rivalry between the <i><a href="/wiki/Voorcompagnie" title="Voorcompagnie">voorcompagnie\xc3\xabn</a></i> undermined the country\'s fragile political unity and economic prosperity, and seriously limited the prospects of competing successfully against other Asian traders from Europe. By attacking the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Union" title="Iberian Union">Luso-Hispanic overseas empire</a>, a large, united company would also help in the ongoing war against the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish Habsburg">Spanish Habsburgs</a>. Initially Van Oldenbarnevelt thought of no more than two or three manned strongholds (Van Deventer 1862, 301), but the Estates General wanted an offensive (Van Brakel 1908, 20-21)."<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-234"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-234">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Richard_Sylla" title="Richard Sylla">Richard Sylla</a> (2015) notes: "In modern history, several nations had what some of us call financial revolutions. These can be thought of as creating in a short period of time all the key components of a modern <a href="/wiki/Financial_system" title="Financial system">financial system</a>. The first was the Dutch Republic four centuries ago."<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-242"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-242">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>\'s own words, \xe2\x80\x9cIts [17th-century Dutch Republic\'s] fisheries, marine, manufactures, surpassed those of any other country. The total capital of the Republic was probably more important than that of all the rest of Europe put together.\xe2\x80\x9d (<i><a href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">Das Kapital</a></i>) <br /> As <a href="/wiki/Witold_Rybczynski" title="Witold Rybczynski">Witold Rybczynski</a> (1987) notes, the Dutch Republic or the United Provinces of the Netherlands, in its Golden Age of the 17th-century, "had few natural resources\xe2\x80\x94no mines, no forests\xe2\x80\x94and what little land there was needed constant protection from the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> But this "low" country surprisingly quickly established itself as a major power. In a short time it became the most advanced shipbuilding nation in the world and developed large naval, fishing, and merchant fleets. (...) The Netherlands introduced many financial innovations that made it a major economic force\xe2\x80\x94and Amsterdam became the world center for international finance. Its manufacturing towns grew so quickly that by the middle of the century the Netherlands had supplanted France as the leading industrial nation of the world."<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-252"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-252">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Hollander_beater" title="Hollander beater">Hollander beater</a> (in the 17th-century) that made the Dutch Republic a major player in global <a href="/wiki/Pulp_and_paper_industry" title="Pulp and paper industry">pulp and paper industry</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-255"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-255">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Wallerstein" title="Immanuel Wallerstein">Immanuel Wallerstein</a> (1980) remarked, the Dutch shipbuilding industry was "of modern dimensions, inclining strongly toward standardised, repetitive methods. It was highly mechanized and used many labor-saving devices \xe2\x80\x94 wind-powered sawmills, powered feeders for saw, block and tackles, great cranes to move heavy timbers \xe2\x80\x94 all of which increased productivity."<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-263"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-263">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Balkenende\'s own words: "Let us be optimistic! Let us say, \xe2\x80\x98It is possible again in The Netherlands!\xe2\x80\x99 That VOC mentality: looking across borders with dynamism!" [translated from the original text in Dutch].<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-265"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-265">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For the Dutch role in the history of capitalism (<a href="/wiki/Modern_capitalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern capitalism">modern capitalism</a> in particular), see: <a href="/wiki/History_of_capitalism#Origins_of_capitalism" title="History of capitalism">History of capitalism#Origins of capitalism</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-275"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-275">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On Chinese role in the history of pre-1600s Taiwan, Laurence G. Thompson (1964) noted, "The most striking fact about the historical knowledge of Formosa is the lack of it in Chinese records. It is truly astonishing that this very large island, so close to the mainland that on exceptionally clear days it may be made out from certain places on the <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fukien</a> coast with the unaided eye, should have remained virtually beyond the ken of Chinese writers down until late <a href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming times</a> (seventeenth century)."<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-281"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-281">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Dutch Republic or officially the <a href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_the_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="United Provinces of the Netherlands">Republic of the Seven United Provinces of the Netherlands</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Republiek_der_Zeven_Verenigde_Nederlanden" class="mw-redirect" title="Republiek der Zeven Verenigde Nederlanden">Republiek der Zeven Verenigde Nederlanden</a></i> or <i>Republiek der Verenigde Nederlanden</i> in Dutch). Weststeijn, Arthur: \xe2\x80\x9cThe VOC as Company-State: Debating Seventeenth Century Dutch Colonial Expansion\xe2\x80\x9d, in <i>Itinerario. International Journal on the History of European Expansion and Global Interaction</i>, 38, 2014, pp. 13\xe2\x80\x9334</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-297"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-297">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See also <a href="/wiki/Jan_Jansz._Weltevree" title="Jan Jansz. Weltevree">Jan Jansz. Weltevree</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-312"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-312">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Including some notable figures of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Netherlandish_cartography" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Age of Netherlandish cartography">Netherlandish school of cartography</a> in its golden age (c. 1570s\xe2\x80\x931670s) like <a href="/wiki/Petrus_Plancius" title="Petrus Plancius">Petrus Plancius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willem_Blaeu" title="Willem Blaeu">Willem Blaeu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Blaeu" class="mw-redirect" title="Johannes Blaeu">Johannes Blaeu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hessel_Gerritsz" title="Hessel Gerritsz">Hessel Gerritsz</a> \xe2\x80\x94 the official cartographers to the VOC.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-347"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-347">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Swedish historian <a href="/wiki/Jan_Glete" title="Jan Glete">Jan Glete</a>\'s words, "From the late sixteenth to the early eighteenth century (...) Dutch maritime activities are normally described as superior to those of other nations and proofs of the Dutch society\xe2\x80\x99s ability to combine technology, entrepreneurship and low transaction costs. The Dutch was in this period the naval enemy or ally of Spain, Portugal, England, France, Denmark-Norway and Sweden. In the naval histories of these countries, the Dutch navy is treated with respect, admiration or envy. In 1639, it won one of the most decisive victories ever achieved in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Downs" title="Battle of the Downs">a major fleet contest against Spain-Portugal in the Channel</a>, and in 1658\xe2\x80\x9359 it <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Sound" title="Battle of the Sound">saved Denmark from possible extinction as an independent state</a> by Sweden. In 1667, it <a href="/wiki/Raid_on_the_Medway" title="Raid on the Medway">attacked the English fleet in its bases</a>, in 1672\xe2\x80\x9373 it waged a very successful defensive campaign against the combined fleets of France and England [the two <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Schooneveld" title="Battle of Schooneveld">battles of Schooneveld</a> <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Texel" title="Battle of Texel">and Texel</a>], and in 1688 it <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">achieved an invasion of England</a> in an excellently administrated surprise mobilisation of a major fleet. In a European perspective, the Dutch navy is a strong candidate for the position as the most successful naval organisation of the seventeenth century."<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-351"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-351">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Zaandam (Saardam) was a historical center of the Dutch Republic\'s well-known shipbuilding industry. The shipbuilding district of Zaandam, in <a href="/wiki/Holland" title="Holland">Holland</a>, was one of the world\'s earliest known heavily <a href="/wiki/Industrialization" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialization">industrialized</a> areas.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-352"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-352">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">n\xc3\xa9e Maria van Aelst, wife of <a href="/wiki/Anthony_van_Diemen" title="Anthony van Diemen">Anthony van Diemen</a> (Anthoonij van Diemen in Dutch), the <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_the_Dutch_East_Indies" title="Governor-General of the Dutch East Indies">Governor-General of the Dutch East Indies</a> in <a href="/wiki/Batavia,_Dutch_East_Indies" title="Batavia, Dutch East Indies">Batavia</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-353"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-353">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The town named after the then governor of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Cape_Colony" title="Dutch Cape Colony">Dutch Cape Colony</a>, Cornelis Jacob van de Graaff, and his wife, whose maiden name was "Reinet".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-354"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-354">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">i.e., first settled or otherwise came into existence.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-355"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-355">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> named after <a href="/wiki/Maurice_of_Nassau" class="mw-redirect" title="Maurice of Nassau">Maurice of Nassau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Orange" title="Prince of Orange">Prince of Orange</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stadtholder" title="Stadtholder">Stadtholder</a> of all the provinces of the Dutch Republic (except for Friesland) from 1585 until his death in 1625.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-356"><span Australia">History of Western Australia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Shipwrecks_of_Western_Australia" title="Shipwrecks of Western Australia">Shipwrecks of Western Australia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Brouwer_Route" The \'Committee for Affairs Relating to East India Trade and Possessions\', instituted by the new Batavian Republic revolutionary government, resulting in the VOC being nationalised on 1 January 1800. The VOC charter, the legal foundation of the enterprise was revoked. Although the state of war in Europe permitted no drastic changes in course as far as shipping and trade to Asia were concerned it spelled the end of the company <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">Balk, L., Van Dijk, F., Kortlang, D., Gaastra, F. The French sent naval expeditions from metropolitan France, which the VOC easily countered. On the other hand, the VOC conquered the important fortress of <a href="/wiki/Pondicherry_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pondicherry (city)">Pondich\xc3\xa9ry</a> after a siege of only 16 days by an expedition of 3,000 men and 19 ships under <a href="/w/index.php?title=Laurens_Pit&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Laurens Pit (page does not exist)">Laurens Pit</a> from <a href="/wiki/Negapatnam" class="mw-redirect" title="Negapatnam">Negapatnam</a> in September 1693. The Dutch then made the defenses of the fortress impregnable, which they came to regret when the Dutch government returned it to the French by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ryswick" title="Treaty of Ryswick">Treaty of Ryswick</a> in exchange for tariff concessions in Europe by the French. Chauhuri and Israel, p 424</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">De Vries and Van der Woude, pp.&#160;433\xe2\x80\x93434</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chaudhuri and Israel, pp.&#160;428\xe2\x80\x93429</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">However, the VOC had been defeated many times before. On the Indian Subcontinent, the EIC had suffered a resounding defeat from the Mughal forces in its <a href="/w/index.php?title=1689_Mughal_War&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="1689 Mughal War (page does not exist)">1689 Mughal War</a>;
